</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Some of the main classes of sulfur-containing organic compounds include the following:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cremlyn_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cremlyn-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Thiol" title="Thiol">Thiols</a> or mercaptans (so called because they capture mercury as <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">chelators</a>) are the sulfur analogs of <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohols</a>; treatment of thiols with base gives <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thiolate" title="Thiolate">thiolate</a> ions.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thioether" title="Thioether">Thioethers</a> are the sulfur analogs of <a href="/wiki/Ether" title="Ether">ethers</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sulfonium" title="Sulfonium">Sulfonium</a> ions have three groups attached to a cationic sulfur center. <a href="/wiki/Dimethylsulfoniopropionate" title="Dimethylsulfoniopropionate">Dimethylsulfoniopropionate</a> (DMSP) is one such compound, important in the marine organic <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_cycle" title="Sulfur cycle">sulfur cycle</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sulfoxide" title="Sulfoxide">Sulfoxides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfone" title="Sulfone">sulfones</a> are thioethers with one and two oxygen atoms attached to the sulfur atom, respectively. The simplest sulfoxide, <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_sulfoxide" title="Dimethyl sulfoxide">dimethyl sulfoxide</a>, is a common solvent; a common sulfone is <a href="/wiki/Sulfolane" title="Sulfolane">sulfolane</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sulfonic_acid" title="Sulfonic acid">Sulfonic acids</a> are used in many detergents.</li></ul>
<p>Compounds with carbon-sulfur multiple bonds are uncommon, an exception being <a href="/wiki/Carbon_disulfide" title="Carbon disulfide">carbon disulfide</a>, a volatile colorless liquid that is structurally similar to carbon dioxide. It is used as a reagent to make the polymer <a href="/wiki/Rayon" title="Rayon">rayon</a> and many organosulfur compounds. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monosulfide" title="Carbon monosulfide">carbon monosulfide</a> is stable only as an extremely dilute gas, found between solar systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Organosulfur compounds are responsible for some of the unpleasant odors of decaying organic matter. They are widely known as the <a href="/wiki/Odorizer" title="Odorizer">odorant</a> in domestic natural gas, garlic odor, and skunk spray. Not all organic sulfur compounds smell unpleasant at all concentrations: the sulfur-containing <a href="/wiki/Terpene" title="Terpene">monoterpenoid</a> (<a href="/wiki/Grapefruit_mercaptan" title="Grapefruit mercaptan">grapefruit mercaptan</a>) in small concentrations is the characteristic scent of grapefruit, but has a generic thiol odor at larger concentrations. <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_mustard" title="Sulfur mustard">Sulfur mustard</a>, a potent <a href="/wiki/Blister_agent" title="Blister agent">vesicant</a>, was <a href="/wiki/Chemical_weapons_in_World_War_I" title="Chemical weapons in World War I">used in World War I</a> as a disabling agent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sulfur-sulfur bonds are a structural component used to stiffen rubber, similar to the disulfide bridges that rigidify proteins (see biological below). In the most common type of industrial "curing" or hardening and strengthening of natural <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rubber" title="Rubber">rubber</a>, elemental sulfur is heated with the rubber to the point that chemical reactions form <a href="/wiki/Disulfide" title="Disulfide">disulfide</a> bridges between <a href="/wiki/Isoprene" title="Isoprene">isoprene</a> units of the polymer. This process, patented in 1843, made rubber a major industrial product, especially in automobile tires. Because of the heat and sulfur, the process was named <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_vulcanization" title="Sulfur vulcanization">vulcanization</a>, after the Roman god of the forge and <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">volcanism</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MODOAzufre.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1384" data-file-width="1853" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/MODOAzufre.jpg/220px-MODOAzufre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/MODOAzufre.jpg/330px-MODOAzufre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/MODOAzufre.jpg/440px-MODOAzufre.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MODOAzufre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pharmaceutical container for sulfur from the first half of the 20th century. From the <a href="/wiki/Museo_del_Objeto_del_Objeto" title="Museo del Objeto del Objeto">Museo del Objeto del Objeto</a> collection</div></div></div>
<p>Being abundantly available in native form, sulfur was known in ancient times and is referred to in the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Genesis</a>). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_translations_of_the_Bible" title="English translations of the Bible">English translations of the Bible</a> commonly referred to burning sulfur as "brimstone", giving rise to the term "<a href="/wiki/Fire_and_brimstone" title="Fire and brimstone">fire-and-brimstone</a>" <a href="/wiki/Sermon" title="Sermon">sermons</a>, in which listeners are reminded of the fate of <a href="/wiki/Damnation" title="Damnation">eternal damnation</a> that await the unbelieving and unrepentant. It is from this part of the Bible that <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">Hell</a> is implied to "smell of sulfur" (likely due to its association with volcanic activity). According to the <a href="/wiki/Ebers_Papyrus" title="Ebers Papyrus">Ebers Papyrus</a>, a sulfur ointment was used in ancient <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> to treat granular eyelids. Sulfur was used for fumigation in preclassical <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> this is mentioned in the <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> discusses sulfur in book 35 of his <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a></i>, saying that its best-known source is the island of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Melos" title="Melos">Melos</a>. He mentions its use for fumigation, medicine, and bleaching cloth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>A natural form of sulfur known as <i>shiliuhuang</i> (石硫黄) was known in China since the 6th century BC and found in <a href="/wiki/Hanzhong" title="Hanzhong">Hanzhong</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yunming_487_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-yunming_487-38">[38]</a></sup> By the 3rd century, the Chinese discovered that sulfur could be extracted from <a href="/wiki/Pyrite" title="Pyrite">pyrite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yunming_487_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-yunming_487-38">[38]</a></sup> Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daoists" title="Daoists">Daoists</a> were interested in sulfur's flammability and its reactivity with certain metals, yet its earliest practical uses were found in <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese medicine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yunming_487_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-yunming_487-38">[38]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> military treatise of 1044 AD described different formulas for Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_powder" title="Black powder">black powder</a>, which is a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">potassium nitrate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">KNO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a>, and sulfur. It remains an ingredient of <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">black gunpowder</a>.
</p><p>Indian alchemists, practitioners of "the science of mercury" (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">sanskrit</a> rasaśāstra, रसशास्त्र), wrote extensively about the use of sulfur in alchemical operations with mercury, from the eighth century AD onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-white-alchemical_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-white-alchemical-39">[39]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Rasa_shastra" title="Rasa shastra">rasaśāstra</a> tradition, sulfur is called "the smelly" (sanskrit gandhaka, गन्धक).
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:243px;max-width:243px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:59px;max-width:59px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:87px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sulphur.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="252" data-file-width="162" height="89" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Sulphur.svg/57px-Sulphur.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Sulphur.svg/86px-Sulphur.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Sulphur.svg/114px-Sulphur.svg.png 2x" width="57"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:90px;max-width:90px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:87px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sulfur_symbol_2.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="100" height="88" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Sulfur_symbol_2.svg/88px-Sulfur_symbol_2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Sulfur_symbol_2.svg/132px-Sulfur_symbol_2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Sulfur_symbol_2.svg/176px-Sulfur_symbol_2.svg.png 2x" width="88"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:88px;max-width:88px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:87px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alchemy_sulfur_symbol.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="146" data-file-width="150" height="84" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Alchemy_sulfur_symbol.svg/86px-Alchemy_sulfur_symbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Alchemy_sulfur_symbol.svg/129px-Alchemy_sulfur_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Alchemy_sulfur_symbol.svg/172px-Alchemy_sulfur_symbol.svg.png 2x" width="86"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Various alchemical symbols for sulfur</div></div></div><p>Early <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemists</a> gave sulfur a unique <a href="/wiki/Alchemical_symbol" title="Alchemical symbol">alchemical symbol</a>, a triangle at the top of a cross. In traditional skin treatment, elemental sulfur was used (mainly in creams) to alleviate such conditions as <a href="/wiki/Scabies" title="Scabies">scabies</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ringworm" title="Ringworm">ringworm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psoriasis" title="Psoriasis">psoriasis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eczema" title="Eczema">eczema</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Acne" title="Acne">acne</a>. The mechanism of action is unknown—though elemental sulfur does oxidize slowly to sulfurous acid, which is (through the action of <a href="/wiki/Sulfite" title="Sulfite">sulfite</a>) a mild reducing and antibacterial agent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_times">Modern times</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Modern times">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soufre_extraction_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1464" data-file-width="1643" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Soufre_extraction_1.jpg/220px-Soufre_extraction_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Soufre_extraction_1.jpg/330px-Soufre_extraction_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Soufre_extraction_1.jpg/440px-Soufre_extraction_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soufre_extraction_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sicilian kiln used to obtain sulfur from volcanic rock</div></div></div>
<p>In 1777, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> helped convince the scientific community that sulfur was an element, not a compound.
</p><p>Sulfur deposits in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> were the dominant source for more than a century. By the late 18th century, about 2,000 tonnes per year of sulfur were imported into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marseilles" title="Marseilles">Marseilles</a>, France, for the production of <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> for use in the <a href="/wiki/Leblanc_process" title="Leblanc process">Leblanc process</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">industrializing</a> Britain, with the repeal of <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">tariffs</a> on salt in 1824, demand for sulfur from Sicily surged upward. The increasing British control and exploitation of the mining, refining, and transportation of the sulfur, coupled with the failure of this lucrative export to transform Sicily's backward and impoverished economy, led to the 'Sulfur Crisis' of 1840, when <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II_of_the_Two_Sicilies" title="Ferdinand II of the Two Sicilies">King Ferdinand II</a> gave a monopoly of the sulfur industry to a French firm, violating an earlier 1816 trade agreement with Britain. A peaceful solution was eventually negotiated by France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1867, elemental sulfur was discovered in underground deposits in <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>. The highly successful <a href="/wiki/Frasch_process" title="Frasch process">Frasch process</a> was developed to extract this resource.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frasch_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frasch-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 18th century, <a href="/wiki/Furniture" title="Furniture">furniture</a> makers used molten sulfur to produce <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_inlay" title="Sulfur inlay">decorative inlays</a> in their craft. Because of the <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> produced during the process of melting sulfur, the craft of sulfur inlays was soon abandoned. Molten sulfur is sometimes still used for setting steel bolts into drilled concrete holes where high shock resistance is desired for floor-mounted equipment attachment points. Pure powdered sulfur was used as a medicinal tonic and laxative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nehb_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nehb-23">[23]</a></sup> With the advent of the <a href="/wiki/Contact_process" title="Contact process">contact process</a>, the majority of sulfur today is used to make <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> for a wide range of uses, particularly fertilizer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spelling_and_etymology">Spelling and etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Spelling and etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Sulfur</i> is derived from the Latin word <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">sulpur</i></i>, which was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenized" title="Hellenized">Hellenized</a> to <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">sulphur</i></i>. The spelling <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">sulfur</i></i> appears toward the end of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical period</a>. (The true Greek word for sulfur, <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">θεῖον</span>, is the source of the international chemical prefix <i><a href="/wiki/Thio-" title="Thio-">thio-</a></i>.) In 12th-century <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_language" title="Anglo-Norman language">Anglo-French</a>, it was <i><i lang="xno" title="Anglo-Norman language text">sulfre</i></i>; in the 14th century the Latin <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">-ph-</i></i> was restored, for <i><i lang="enm" title="Middle English language text">sulphre</i></i>; and by the 15th century the full Latin spelling was restored, for <i>sulfur, sulphur</i>. The parallel <i>f~ph</i> spellings continued in Britain until the 19th century, when the word was standardized as <i>sulphur</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-47">[47]</a></sup> <i>Sulfur</i> was the form chosen in the United States, whereas Canada uses both. The <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">IUPAC</a> adopted the spelling <i>sulfur</i> in 1990, as did the Nomenclature Committee of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Chemistry" title="Royal Society of Chemistry">Royal Society of Chemistry</a> in 1992, restoring the spelling <i>sulfur</i> to Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Oxford Dictionaries note that "in chemistry and other technical uses ... the <i>-f-</i> spelling is now the standard form for this and related words in British as well as US contexts, and is increasingly used in general contexts as well."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bergelut_dengan_asap_nan_beracun.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="1656" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Bergelut_dengan_asap_nan_beracun.jpg/220px-Bergelut_dengan_asap_nan_beracun.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Bergelut_dengan_asap_nan_beracun.jpg/330px-Bergelut_dengan_asap_nan_beracun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Bergelut_dengan_asap_nan_beracun.jpg/440px-Bergelut_dengan_asap_nan_beracun.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bergelut_dengan_asap_nan_beracun.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Traditional sulfur mining at <a href="/wiki/Ijen" title="Ijen">Ijen Volcano</a>, East Java, Indonesia. This image shows the dangerous and rugged conditions the miners face, including toxic smoke and high drops, as well as their lack of protective equipment. The pipes over which they are standing are for condensing sulfur vapors.</div></div></div>
<p>Sulfur may be found by itself and historically was usually obtained in this form; pyrite has also been a source of sulfur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In volcanic regions in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, in ancient times, it was found on the surface of the Earth, and the <i>"Sicilian process"</i> was used: sulfur deposits were piled and stacked in brick kilns built on sloping hillsides, with airspaces between them. Then, some sulfur was pulverized, spread over the stacked ore and ignited, causing the free sulfur to melt down the hills. Eventually the surface-borne deposits played out, and miners excavated veins that ultimately dotted the Sicilian landscape with labyrinthine mines. Mining was unmechanized and labor-intensive, with pickmen freeing the ore from the rock, and mine-boys or <i><a href="/wiki/Carusu" title="Carusu">carusi</a></i> carrying baskets of ore to the surface, often through a mile or more of tunnels. Once the ore was at the surface, it was reduced and extracted in smelting ovens. The conditions in Sicilian sulfur mines were horrific, prompting <a href="/wiki/Booker_T._Washington" title="Booker T. Washington">Booker T. Washington</a> to write "I am not prepared just now to say to what extent I believe in a physical hell in the next world, but a sulphur mine in Sicily is about the nearest thing to hell that I expect to see in this life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Elemental sulfur was extracted from <a href="/wiki/Salt_dome" title="Salt dome">salt domes</a> (in which it sometimes occurs in nearly pure form) until the late 20th century. Sulfur is now  produced as a side product of other industrial processes such as in oil refining, in which sulfur is undesired. As a mineral, native sulfur under salt domes is thought to be a fossil mineral resource, produced by the action of ancient bacteria on sulfate deposits. It was removed from such salt-dome mines mainly by the <a href="/wiki/Frasch_process" title="Frasch process">Frasch process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nehb_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nehb-23">[23]</a></sup> In this method, superheated water was pumped into a native sulfur deposit to melt the sulfur, and then compressed air returned the 99.5% pure melted product to the surface. Throughout the 20th century this procedure produced elemental sulfur that required no further purification. Due to a limited number of such sulfur deposits and the high cost of working them, this process for mining sulfur has not been employed in a major way anywhere in the world since 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-desulf1_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-desulf1-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-desulf2_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-desulf2-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlbertaSulfurAtVancouverBC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1350" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/AlbertaSulfurAtVancouverBC.jpg/220px-AlbertaSulfurAtVancouverBC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/AlbertaSulfurAtVancouverBC.jpg/330px-AlbertaSulfurAtVancouverBC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/AlbertaSulfurAtVancouverBC.jpg/440px-AlbertaSulfurAtVancouverBC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlbertaSulfurAtVancouverBC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sulfur recovered from hydrocarbons in <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, stockpiled for shipment in North Vancouver, British Columbia</div></div></div>
<p>Today, sulfur is produced from petroleum, <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, and related fossil resources, from which it is obtained mainly as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organosulfur_compound" title="Organosulfur compound">Organosulfur compounds</a>, undesirable impurities in petroleum, may be upgraded by subjecting them to <a href="/wiki/Hydrodesulfurization" title="Hydrodesulfurization">hydrodesulfurization</a>, which cleaves the C–S bonds:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-desulf1_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-desulf1-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-desulf2_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-desulf2-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>R-S-R + 2 H<sub>2</sub> → 2 RH + H<sub>2</sub>S</dd></dl>
<p>The resulting hydrogen sulfide from this process, and also as it occurs in natural gas, is converted into elemental sulfur by the <a href="/wiki/Claus_process" title="Claus process">Claus process</a>. This process entails oxidation of some hydrogen sulfide to sulfur dioxide and then the <a href="/wiki/Comproportionation" title="Comproportionation">comproportionation</a> of the two:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-desulf1_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-desulf1-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-desulf2_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-desulf2-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>3 O<sub>2</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>S → 2 SO<sub>2</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd>
<dd>SO<sub>2</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>S → 3 S + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sulfur_price_world_production.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="663" data-file-width="1251" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Sulfur_price_world_production.svg/220px-Sulfur_price_world_production.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Sulfur_price_world_production.svg/330px-Sulfur_price_world_production.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Sulfur_price_world_production.svg/440px-Sulfur_price_world_production.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sulfur_price_world_production.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Production and price (US market) of elemental sulfur</div></div></div>
<p>Owing to the high sulfur content of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athabasca_Oil_Sands" title="Athabasca Oil Sands">Athabasca Oil Sands</a>, stockpiles of elemental sulfur from this process now exist throughout <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atha_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atha-54">[54]</a></sup> Another way of storing sulfur is as a <a href="/wiki/Binder_(material)" title="Binder (material)">binder</a> for concrete, the resulting product having many desirable properties (see <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_concrete" title="Sulfur concrete">sulfur concrete</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Sulfur is still mined from surface deposits in poorer nations with volcanoes, such as Indonesia, and worker conditions have not improved much since Booker T. Washington's days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>The world production of sulfur in 2011 amounted to 69 million tonnes (Mt), with more than 15 countries contributing more than 1 Mt each. Countries producing more than 5 Mt are China (9.6), US (8.8), Canada (7.1) and Russia (7.1).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Production has been slowly increasing from 1900 to 2010; the price was unstable in the 1980s and around 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sulfuric_acid">Sulfuric acid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sulfuric acid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Elemental sulfur is used mainly as a precursor to other chemicals. Approximately 85% (1989) is converted to <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">H</a><sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>):
</p>
<dl><dd>2 S + 3 O<sub>2</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O → 2 H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2000sulphuric_acid.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/2000sulphuric_acid.PNG/220px-2000sulphuric_acid.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/2000sulphuric_acid.PNG/330px-2000sulphuric_acid.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/2000sulphuric_acid.PNG/440px-2000sulphuric_acid.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2000sulphuric_acid.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sulfuric acid production in 2000</div></div></div></dd></dl>
<p>In 2010, the United States produced more sulfuric acid than any other inorganic industrial chemical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-58">[58]</a></sup> The principal use for the acid is the extraction of phosphate ores for the production of fertilizer manufacturing. Other applications of sulfuric acid include oil refining, wastewater processing, and mineral extraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nehb_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nehb-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_important_sulfur_chemistry">Other important sulfur chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Other important sulfur chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfur reacts directly with methane to give <a href="/wiki/Carbon_disulfide" title="Carbon disulfide">carbon disulfide</a>, used to manufacture <a href="/wiki/Cellophane" title="Cellophane">cellophane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rayon" title="Rayon">rayon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nehb_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nehb-23">[23]</a></sup> One of the uses of elemental sulfur is in <a href="/wiki/Vulcanization" title="Vulcanization">vulcanization</a> of rubber, where <a href="/wiki/Polysulfide" title="Polysulfide">polysulfide</a> chains crosslink organic polymers. Large quantities of <a href="/wiki/Sulfite" title="Sulfite">sulfites</a> are used to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bleach_(chemical)" title="Bleach (chemical)">bleach</a> <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a> and to preserve dried <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a>. Many <a href="/wiki/Surfactant" title="Surfactant">surfactants</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detergents" title="Detergents">detergents</a> (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sodium_lauryl_sulfate" title="Sodium lauryl sulfate">sodium lauryl sulfate</a>) are sulfate derivatives. <a href="/wiki/Calcium_sulfate" title="Calcium sulfate">Calcium sulfate</a>, gypsum, (CaSO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O) is mined on the scale of 100 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> each year for use in <a href="/wiki/Portland_cement" title="Portland cement">Portland cement</a> and fertilizers.
</p><p>When silver-based <a href="/wiki/Photography" title="Photography">photography</a> was widespread, sodium and ammonium <a href="/wiki/Sodium_thiosulfate" title="Sodium thiosulfate">thiosulfate</a> were widely used as "fixing agents." Sulfur is a component of <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> ("black powder").
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertilizer">Fertilizer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Fertilizer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfur is increasingly used as a component of <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>. The most important form of sulfur for fertilizer is the mineral <a href="/wiki/Calcium_sulfate" title="Calcium sulfate">calcium sulfate</a>. Elemental sulfur is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophobic" title="Hydrophobic">hydrophobic</a> (not soluble in water) and cannot be used directly by plants. Over time, soil bacteria can convert it to soluble derivatives, which can then be used by plants. Sulfur improves the efficiency of other essential plant nutrients, particularly nitrogen and phosphorus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Biologically produced sulfur particles are naturally hydrophilic due to a biopolymer coating and are easier to disperse over the land in a spray of diluted slurry, resulting in a faster uptake.
</p><p>The botanical requirement for sulfur equals or exceeds the requirement for phosphorus. <a href="/wiki/Plant_nutrition" title="Plant nutrition">It is an essential nutrient for plant</a> growth, root nodule formation of legumes, and immunity and defense systems. Sulfur deficiency has become widespread in many countries in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Because atmospheric inputs of sulfur continue to decrease, the deficit in the sulfur input/output is likely to increase unless sulfur fertilizers are used.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fine_chemicals">Fine chemicals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Fine chemicals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malathion-3D-vdW.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="785" data-file-width="1100" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Malathion-3D-vdW.png/220px-Malathion-3D-vdW.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Malathion-3D-vdW.png/330px-Malathion-3D-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Malathion-3D-vdW.png/440px-Malathion-3D-vdW.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Malathion-3D-vdW.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A molecular model of the pesticide <a href="/wiki/Malathion" title="Malathion">malathion</a></div></div></div>
<p>Organosulfur compounds are used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical" title="Pharmaceutical">pharmaceuticals</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyestuff" title="Dyestuff">dyestuffs</a>, and agrochemicals. Many drugs contain sulfur, early examples being antibacterial <a href="/wiki/Sulfonamide_(medicine)" title="Sulfonamide (medicine)">sulfonamides</a>, known as <i>sulfa drugs</i>. Sulfur is a part of many bacterial defense molecules. Most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%92-lactam" title="Β-lactam">β-lactam</a> antibiotics, including the <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillins</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cephalosporins" title="Cephalosporins">cephalosporins</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Monolactam&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Monolactam (page does not exist)">monolactams</a> contain sulfur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cremlyn_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cremlyn-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Magnesium_sulfate" title="Magnesium sulfate">Magnesium sulfate</a>, known as Epsom salts when in hydrated crystal form, can be used as a <a href="/wiki/Laxative" title="Laxative">laxative</a>, a bath additive, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exfoliant" title="Exfoliant">exfoliant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> supplement for plants, or (when in dehydrated form) as a <a href="/wiki/Desiccant" title="Desiccant">desiccant</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fungicide_and_pesticide">Fungicide and pesticide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Fungicide and pesticide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sulphur_Candle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="422" data-file-width="343" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Sulphur_Candle.jpg/170px-Sulphur_Candle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Sulphur_Candle.jpg/255px-Sulphur_Candle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Sulphur_Candle.jpg/340px-Sulphur_Candle.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sulphur_Candle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sulfur candle originally sold for home fumigation</div></div></div>
<p>Elemental sulfur is one of the oldest fungicides and pesticides. "Dusting sulfur", elemental sulfur in powdered form, is a common fungicide for grapes, strawberry, many vegetables and several other crops. It has a good efficacy against a wide range of powdery mildew diseases as well as black spot. In organic production, sulfur is the most important fungicide. It is the only fungicide used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_agriculture" title="Organic agriculture">organically</a> farmed apple production against the main disease <a href="/wiki/Apple_scab" title="Apple scab">apple scab</a> under colder conditions. Biosulfur (biologically produced elemental sulfur with hydrophilic characteristics) can also be used for these applications.
</p><p>Standard-formulation dusting sulfur is applied to crops with a sulfur duster or from a dusting plane. Wettable sulfur is the commercial name for dusting sulfur formulated with additional ingredients to make it water <a href="/wiki/Miscibility" title="Miscibility">miscible</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> It has similar applications and is used as a <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicide</a> against <a href="/wiki/Mildew" title="Mildew">mildew</a> and other mold-related problems with plants and soil.
</p><p>Elemental sulfur powder is used as an "<a href="/wiki/Organic_farming" title="Organic farming">organic</a>" (i.e., "green") <a href="/wiki/Insecticide" title="Insecticide">insecticide</a> (actually an <a href="/wiki/Acaricide" title="Acaricide">acaricide</a>) against <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">ticks</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mites" title="Mites">mites</a>. A common method of application is dusting the clothing or limbs with sulfur powder.
</p><p>A diluted solution of <a href="/wiki/Lime_sulfur" title="Lime sulfur">lime sulfur</a> (made by combining <a href="/wiki/Calcium_hydroxide" title="Calcium hydroxide">calcium hydroxide</a> with elemental sulfur in water) is used as a dip for pets to destroy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ringworm" title="Ringworm">ringworm (fungus)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mange" title="Mange">mange</a>, and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cutaneous_conditions" title="Cutaneous conditions">dermatoses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasites</a>.
</p><p>Sulfur candles of almost pure sulfur were burned to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fumigant" title="Fumigant">fumigate</a> structures and wine barrels, but are now considered too toxic for residences.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bactericide_in_winemaking_and_food_preservation">Bactericide in winemaking and food preservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Bactericide in winemaking and food preservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> gas addition (or equivalent <a href="/wiki/Potassium_metabisulfite" title="Potassium metabisulfite">potassium metabisulfite</a> addition) to fermented wine to produce traces of <a href="/wiki/Sulfurous_acid" title="Sulfurous acid">sulfurous acid</a> (produced when SO<sub>2</sub> reacts with water) and its <a href="/wiki/Sulfite" title="Sulfite">sulfite</a> salts in the mixture, has been called "the most powerful tool in winemaking."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> After the yeast-fermentation stage in <a href="/wiki/Winemaking" title="Winemaking">winemaking</a>, sulfites absorb oxygen and inhibit <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism">aerobic</a> bacterial growth that otherwise would turn ethanol into acetic acid, souring the wine. Without this preservative step, indefinite refrigeration of the product before consumption is usually required. Similar methods go back into antiquity but modern historical mentions of the practice go to the fifteenth century. The practice is used by large industrial wine producers and small organic wine producers alike.
</p><p>Sulfur dioxide and various sulfites have been used for their antioxidant antibacterial preservative properties in many other parts of the food industry. The practice has declined since reports of an allergy-like reaction of some persons to sulfites in foods.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmaceuticals">Pharmaceuticals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Pharmaceuticals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Sulfur (specifically <a href="/wiki/Octasulfur" title="Octasulfur">octasulfur</a>, S<sub>8</sub>) is used in pharmaceutical skin preparations for the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Acne" title="Acne">acne</a> and other conditions. It acts as a <a href="/wiki/Keratolytic" title="Keratolytic">keratolytic</a> agent and also kills bacteria, fungi, <a href="/wiki/Scabies" title="Scabies">scabies</a> mites and other parasites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hager_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hager-66">[66]</a></sup> Precipitated sulfur and colloidal sulfur are used, in form of <a href="/wiki/Lotion" title="Lotion">lotions</a>, creams, powders, soaps, and bath additives, for the treatment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acne_vulgaris" title="Acne vulgaris">acne vulgaris</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acne_rosacea" title="Acne rosacea">acne rosacea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Seborrhoeic_dermatitis" title="Seborrhoeic dermatitis">seborrhoeic dermatitis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arzneibuch-Kommentar_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arzneibuch-Kommentar-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Common adverse effects include irritation of the skin at the application site, such as dryness, stinging, itching and peeling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTM_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-MTM-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanism_of_action">Mechanism of action</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Mechanism of action">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b> with: the keratolysis mechanism. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(March 2014)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Sulfur is converted to hydrogen sulfide (H<sub>2</sub>S) through <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduction</a>, partly by bacteria. H<sub>2</sub>S kills bacteria (possibly including <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propionibacterium_acnes" title="Propionibacterium acnes">Propionibacterium acnes</a></i> which plays a role in acne,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drugs.com_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drugs.com-69">[69]</a></sup>) fungi, and parasites such as scabies mites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hager_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hager-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Furniture">Furniture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Furniture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_inlay" title="Sulfur inlay">Sulfur inlay</a></div>
<p>Sulfur can be used to create decorative <a href="/wiki/Inlay" title="Inlay">inlays</a> in wooden furniture. After a design has been cut into the wood, molten sulfur is poured in and then scraped away so it is flush. Sulfur inlays were particularly popular in the late 18th and early 19th centuries, notably amongst <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_German" title="Pennsylvania German">Pennsylvania German</a> cabinetmakers. The practice soon died out, as less toxic and flammable substances were substituted. However, some modern craftsmen have occasionally revived the technique in the creation of replica pieces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Biological role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protein_and_organic_cofactors">Protein and organic cofactors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Protein and organic cofactors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfur is an essential component of all living <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>. It is either the seventh or eighth most abundant element in the human body by weight, about equal in abundance to <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, and slightly greater than sodium and chlorine. A 70 kg (150 lb) human body contains about 140 grams of sulfur.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> <a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methionine" title="Methionine">methionine</a> contain most of the sulfur, and the element is present in all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polypeptide" title="Polypeptide">polypeptides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> that contain these amino acids. In humans, methionine is an essential amino acid that must be ingested. However, save for the vitamins <a href="/wiki/Biotin" title="Biotin">biotin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thiamine" title="Thiamine">thiamine</a>, cysteine and all sulfur-containing compounds in the human body can be synthesized from methionine. The enzyme <a href="/wiki/Sulfite_oxidase" title="Sulfite oxidase">sulfite oxidase</a> is needed for the metabolism of methionine and cysteine in humans and animals.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disulfide_bond" title="Disulfide bond">Disulfide bonds</a> (S-S bonds) between cysteine residues in peptide chains are very important in protein assembly and structure. These covalent bonds between peptide chains confer extra toughness and rigidity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehn_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lehn-72">[72]</a></sup> For example, the high strength of feathers and hair is due in part to the high content of S-S bonds with cysteine and sulfur. Eggs are high in sulfur to nourish feather formation in chicks, and the characteristic odor of rotting eggs is due to <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>. The high disulfide bond content of hair and feathers contributes to their indigestibility and to their characteristic disagreeable odor when burned.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Homocysteine" title="Homocysteine">Homocysteine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taurine" title="Taurine">taurine</a> are other sulfur-containing acids that are similar in structure, but not coded by <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>, and are not part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_structure" title="Primary structure">primary structure</a> of proteins. Many important cellular enzymes use prosthetic groups ending with -SH moieties to handle reactions involving acyl-containing biochemicals: two common examples from basic metabolism are <a href="/wiki/Coenzyme_A" title="Coenzyme A">coenzyme A</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha-lipoic_acid" title="Alpha-lipoic acid">alpha-lipoic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehn_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lehn-72">[72]</a></sup> Two of the 13 classical vitamins, <a href="/wiki/Biotin" title="Biotin">biotin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thiamine" title="Thiamine">thiamine</a>, contain sulfur, with the latter being named for its sulfur content.
</p><p>In intracellular chemistry, sulfur operates as a carrier of reducing hydrogen and its electrons for cellular repair of oxidation. Reduced <a href="/wiki/Glutathione" title="Glutathione">glutathione</a>, a sulfur-containing tripeptide, is a reducing agent through its sulfhydryl (-SH) moiety derived from <a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Thioredoxin" title="Thioredoxin">thioredoxins</a>, a class of small proteins essential to all known life, use neighboring pairs of reduced cysteines to work as general protein reducing agents, with similar effect.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Methanogenesis" title="Methanogenesis">Methanogenesis</a>, the route to most of the world's methane, is a multistep biochemical transformation of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>. This conversion requires several organosulfur cofactors. These include <a href="/wiki/Coenzyme_M" title="Coenzyme M">coenzyme M</a>, CH<sub>3</sub>SCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, the immediate precursor to <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metalloproteins_and_inorganic_cofactors">Metalloproteins and inorganic cofactors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Metalloproteins and inorganic cofactors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Inorganic sulfur forms a part of <a href="/wiki/Iron%E2%80%93sulfur_cluster" title="Iron–sulfur cluster">iron–sulfur clusters</a> as well as many copper, nickel, and iron proteins. Most pervasive are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrodoxin" title="Ferrodoxin">ferrodoxins</a>, which serve as electron shuttles in cells. In bacteria, the important <a href="/wiki/Nitrogenase" title="Nitrogenase">nitrogenase</a> enzymes contains an Fe–Mo–S cluster and is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a> that performs the important function of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">nitrogen fixation</a>, converting atmospheric nitrogen to ammonia that can be used by microorganisms and plants to make proteins, DNA, RNA, alkaloids, and the other organic nitrogen compounds necessary for life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FdRedox.png"><img alt="FdRedox.png" data-file-height="434" data-file-width="1904" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/FdRedox.png/500px-FdRedox.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/FdRedox.png/750px-FdRedox.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/FdRedox.png/1000px-FdRedox.png 2x" width="500"/></a></div></div></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sulfur_metabolism_and_the_sulfur_cycle">Sulfur metabolism and the sulfur cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Sulfur metabolism and the sulfur cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_metabolism" title="Sulfur metabolism">Sulfur metabolism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_cycle" title="Sulfur cycle">Sulfur cycle</a></div>
<p>The sulfur cycle was the first of the <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">biogeochemical cycles</a> to be discovered. In the 1880s, while studying <a href="/wiki/Beggiatoa" title="Beggiatoa">Beggiatoa</a> (a bacterium living in a sulfur rich environment), <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Winogradsky" title="Sergei Winogradsky">Sergei Winogradsky</a> found that it oxidized <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> (H<sub>2</sub>S) as an energy source, forming intracellular sulfur droplets. Winogradsky referred to this form of metabolism as inorgoxidation (oxidation of inorganic compounds). He continued to study it together with <a href="/wiki/Selman_Waksman" title="Selman Waksman">Selman Waksman</a> until the 1950s.
</p><p>Sulfur oxidizers can use as energy sources reduced sulfur compounds, including hydrogen sulfide, elemental sulfur, <a href="/wiki/Sulfite" title="Sulfite">sulfite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thiosulfate" title="Thiosulfate">thiosulfate</a>, and various polythionates (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Tetrathionate" title="Tetrathionate">tetrathionate</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> They depend on enzymes such as <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxygenase" title="Sulfur dioxygenase">sulfur oxygenase</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfite_oxidase" title="Sulfite oxidase">sulfite oxidase</a> to oxidize sulfur to sulfate. Some <a href="/wiki/Lithotroph" title="Lithotroph">lithotrophs</a> can even use the energy contained in sulfur compounds to produce sugars, a process known as <a href="/wiki/Chemosynthesis" title="Chemosynthesis">chemosynthesis</a>. Some <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> use hydrogen sulfide in place of water as the <a href="/wiki/Electron_donor" title="Electron donor">electron donor</a> in chemosynthesis, a process similar to <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> that produces sugars and utilizes oxygen as the <a href="/wiki/Electron_acceptor" title="Electron acceptor">electron acceptor</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Green_sulfur_bacteria" title="Green sulfur bacteria">green sulfur bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Purple_sulfur_bacteria" title="Purple sulfur bacteria">purple sulfur bacteria</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Lithotroph" title="Lithotroph">lithotrophs</a> use elemental oxygen to carry out such oxidization of hydrogen sulfide to produce elemental sulfur (S<sup>0</sup>), oxidation state = 0. Primitive bacteria that live around deep ocean <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">volcanic vents</a> oxidize hydrogen sulfide in this way with oxygen; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_tube_worm" title="Giant tube worm">giant tube worm</a> is an example of a large organism that uses hydrogen sulfide (via bacteria) as food to be oxidized.
</p><p>The so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulfate-reducing_bacteria" title="Sulfate-reducing bacteria">sulfate-reducing bacteria</a>, by contrast, "breathe sulfate" instead of oxygen. They use organic compounds or molecular hydrogen as the energy source. They use sulfur as the electron acceptor, and reduce various oxidized sulfur compounds back into sulfide, often into hydrogen sulfide. They can grow on other partially oxidized sulfur compounds (e.g. thiosulfates, thionates, polysulfides, sulfites). The hydrogen sulfide produced by these bacteria is responsible for some of the smell of intestinal gases (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flatus" title="Flatus">flatus</a>) and decomposition products.
</p><p>Sulfur is absorbed by <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> <a href="/wiki/Root" title="Root">roots</a> from soil as <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfate</a> and transported as a phosphate ester. Sulfate is reduced to sulfide via sulfite before it is incorporated into <a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a> and other organosulfur compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heldt_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heldt-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>SO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup> → SO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> → H<sub>2</sub>S → cysteine → methionine</dd></dl>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acid_rain_woods1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Acid_rain_woods1.JPG/220px-Acid_rain_woods1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Acid_rain_woods1.JPG/330px-Acid_rain_woods1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Acid_rain_woods1.JPG/440px-Acid_rain_woods1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acid_rain_woods1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Effect of acid rain on a forest, Jizera Mountains, Czech Republic</div></div></div>
<p>Elemental sulfur is non-toxic, as are most of the soluble <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfate</a> salts, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epsom_salt" title="Epsom salt">Epsom salts</a>. Soluble sulfate salts are poorly absorbed and laxative. When injected parenterally, they are freely filtered by the kidneys and eliminated with very little toxicity in multi-gram amounts.
</p><p>When sulfur burns in air, it produces <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a>. In water, this gas produces sulfurous acid and sulfites; sulfites are antioxidants that inhibit growth of aerobic bacteria and a useful <a href="/wiki/Food_additive" title="Food additive">food additive</a> in small amounts. At high concentrations these acids harm the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_lungs" title="Human lungs">lungs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_eyes" title="Human eyes">eyes</a> or other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_tissue" title="Biological tissue">tissues</a>. In organisms without lungs such as insects or plants, sulfite in high concentration prevents <a href="/wiki/Respiration_(physiology)" title="Respiration (physiology)">respiration</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sulfur_trioxide" title="Sulfur trioxide">Sulfur trioxide</a> (made by catalysis from sulfur dioxide) and <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> are similarly highly acidic and corrosive in the presence of water. Sulfuric acid is a strong dehydrating agent that can strip available water molecules and water components from sugar and organic tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>The burning of <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> by industry and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_plants" title="Power plants">power plants</a> generates sulfur dioxide (SO<sub>2</sub>) that reacts with atmospheric water and oxygen to produce sulfuric acid (H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Sulfurous_acid" title="Sulfurous acid">sulfurous acid</a> (H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>). These acids are components of <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>, lowering the <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> and freshwater bodies, sometimes resulting in substantial damage to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment (biophysical)">environment</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_weathering" title="Chemical weathering">chemical weathering</a> of statues and structures. Fuel standards increasingly require that fuel producers extract sulfur from <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a> to prevent acid rain formation. This extracted and refined sulfur represents a large portion of sulfur production. In coal-fired power plants, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flue_gases" title="Flue gases">flue gases</a> are sometimes purified. More modern power plants that use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthesis_gas" title="Synthesis gas">synthesis gas</a> extract the sulfur before they burn the gas.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">Hydrogen sulfide</a> is as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic" title="Toxic">toxic</a> as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">hydrogen cyanide</a>, and kills by the same mechanism (inhibition of the respiratory enzyme <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cytochrome_oxidase" title="Cytochrome oxidase">cytochrome oxidase</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> though hydrogen sulfide is less likely to cause surprise poisonings from small inhaled amounts because of its disagreeable odor. Hydrogen sulfide quickly deadens the sense of smell and a victim may breathe increasing quantities without noticing the increase until severe symptoms cause death. Dissolved <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfide</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrosulfide" title="Hydrosulfide">hydrosulfide</a> salts are toxic by the same mechanism.
</p>
